The Song occupies the east half of what constitutes the territory of the modern People's Republic of China, except for the northernmost areas (modern Inner Mongolia and above). Western Xia occupies a small strip of land surrounding a river in what is now Inner Mongolia and Ningxia, and the Liao occupy a large section of what is today north-east China." data-file-height="825" data-file-width="840" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/China_-_Song_Dynasty-en.svg/250px-China_-_Song_Dynasty-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/China_-_Song_Dynasty-en.svg/375px-China_-_Song_Dynasty-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/China_-_Song_Dynasty-en.svg/500px-China_-_Song_Dynasty-en.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div>

<td colspan="8" style="line-height: 125%; font-size: 80%; padding: 2px 2px 2px 3px; border-top: 1px solid #777; background: #FEC; text-align:center;">

<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_China" title="Template:History of China"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_China" title="Template talk:History of China"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li>

<p>The <b>Song dynasty</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">宋朝</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Sòng cháo</span></i>; 960–1279) was an era of Chinese history that began in 960 and continued until 1279. It succeeded the <a href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties_and_Ten_Kingdoms_period" title="Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period">Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period</a>, coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a> dynasties, and was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>. It was the first government in world history to issue <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">banknotes</a> or true paper money nationally and the first Chinese government to establish <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">a permanent standing navy</a>. This dynasty also saw the first known use of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>, as well as the first discernment of <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">true north</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>.</p>
<p>The Song dynasty is divided into two distinct periods, Northern and Southern. During the <b>Northern Song</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">北宋</span>; 960–1127), the Song capital was in the northern city of Bianjing (now <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng" title="Kaifeng">Kaifeng</a>) and the dynasty controlled most of what is now Eastern China. The <b>Southern Song</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">南宋</span>; 1127–1279) refers to the period after the Song lost control of its northern half to the Jurchen <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Jin%E2%80%93Song_Wars" title="Jin–Song Wars">Jin–Song Wars</a>. During this time, the Song court retreated south of the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> and established its capital at Lin'an (now <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>). Although the Song dynasty had lost control of the traditional "birthplace of Chinese civilization" along the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>, the Song economy was still strong, as the Southern Song Empire contained a large population and productive agricultural land. The Southern Song dynasty considerably bolstered its <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">naval</a> strength to defend its waters and land borders and to conduct <a href="/wiki/Ancient_maritime_history#China" title="Ancient maritime history">maritime</a> missions abroad.</p>
<p>To repel the Jin, and later the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, the Song developed revolutionary new military technology augmented by the use of gunpowder. In 1234, the Jin dynasty was conquered by the Mongols, who took control of northern China, maintaining uneasy relations with the Southern Song. <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a>, the fourth <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Great Khan</a> of the Mongol Empire, died in 1259 while besieging the city of <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a>. His younger brother <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> was proclaimed the new Great Khan, though his claim was only partially recognized by the Mongols in the west. In 1271, Kublai Khan was proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor of China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi1988115_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi1988115-3">[2]</a></sup> After two decades of sporadic warfare, Kublai Khan's armies <a href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Mongol conquest of the Song dynasty">conquered the Song dynasty</a> in 1279. The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_China" title="Mongol invasion of China">Mongol invasion</a> led to a reunification under the Yuan dynasty (1271–1368).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198876_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198876-4">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The population of China doubled in size during the 10th and 11th centuries. This growth was made possible by expanded rice cultivation in central and southern Song, the use of early-ripening rice from south-east and southern Asia, and the production of widespread food surpluses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199896_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199896-6">[5]</a></sup> The Northern Song census recorded a population of roughly 50 million, much like the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> dynasties. This data is found in the <i><a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Standard Histories</a></i>. However, it is estimated that the Northern Song had a population of some 100 million people, and 200 million by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVeeckPannellSmithHuang2007103.E2.80.93104_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVeeckPannellSmithHuang2007103.E2.80.93104-7">[6]</a></sup> This dramatic increase of population fomented an <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Economy of the Song dynasty">economic revolution in pre-modern China</a>. The expansion of the population, growth of cities, and the emergence of a national economy led to the gradual withdrawal of the central government from direct involvement in economic affairs. The lower gentry assumed a larger role in grassroots administration and local affairs. Appointed officials in county and provincial centers relied upon the scholarly gentry for their services, sponsorship, and local supervision.</p>
<p>Social life during the Song was vibrant. Citizens gathered to view and trade precious artworks, the populace intermingled at public festivals and private clubs, and cities had lively entertainment quarters. The spread of literature and knowledge was enhanced by the rapid expansion of <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a> and the 11th-century invention of <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable-type</a> printing. Technology, science, philosophy, mathematics, and engineering flourished over the course of the Song. Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Cheng_Yi_(philosopher)" title="Cheng Yi (philosopher)">Cheng Yi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> reinvigorated <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> with new commentary, infused with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> ideals, and emphasized a new organization of classic texts that brought out the core doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>. Although the institution of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">civil service examinations</a> had existed since the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>, it became much more prominent in the Song period. The officials who gained power by succeeding in the exams became a leading factor in the shift from a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">military-aristocratic</a> elite to a <a href="/wiki/Scholar-official" title="Scholar-official">bureaucratic</a> elite.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="History of the Song dynasty">History of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_emperors_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="List of emperors of the Song dynasty">List of emperors of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Song_dynasty" title="Islam during the Song dynasty">Islam during the Song dynasty</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Song.2C_960.E2.80.931126"><span id="Northern_Song.2C_960-1127"></span>Northern Song, 960–1126</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Northern Song, 960–1126">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Northern Song" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Northern_Song_(disambiguation)" title="Northern Song (disambiguation)">Northern Song (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizu_of_Song" title="Emperor Taizu of Song">Emperor Taizu of Song</a> (r. 960–976) unified the empire by conquering other lands during his reign, ending the upheaval of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties_and_Ten_Kingdoms_period" title="Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period">Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng" title="Kaifeng">Kaifeng</a>, he established a strong central government over the empire. He ensured administrative stability by promoting the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">civil service examination</a> system of drafting state <a href="/wiki/Bureaucrat" title="Bureaucrat">bureaucrats</a> by skill and merit (instead of <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocratic</a> or military position) and promoted projects that ensured efficiency in communication throughout the empire. In one such project, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_cartography" title="Chinese cartography">cartographers</a> created detailed maps of each province and city that were then collected in a large <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b518_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b518-8">[7]</a></sup> Emperor Taizu also promoted groundbreaking scientific and technological innovations by supporting such works as the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_clock" title="Astronomical clock">astronomical</a> <a href="/wiki/Clock_tower" title="Clock tower">clock tower</a> designed and built by the engineer <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Sixun" title="Zhang Sixun">Zhang Sixun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c469.E2.80.93471_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c469.E2.80.93471-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Song_Taizu.jpg"><img alt="Painted image of a portly man sitting in a red throne-chair with dragon-head decorations, wearing white silk robes, black shoes, and a black hat, and sporting a black moustache and goatee." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="775" data-file-width="698" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Song_Taizu.jpg/170px-Song_Taizu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Song_Taizu.jpg/255px-Song_Taizu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Song_Taizu.jpg/340px-Song_Taizu.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Song_Taizu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizu_of_Song" title="Emperor Taizu of Song">Emperor Taizu of Song</a> (r. 960–976), a court portrait painting</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Song court maintained diplomatic relations with <a href="/wiki/Chola_dynasty" title="Chola dynasty">Chola India</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> of Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Srivijaya" title="Srivijaya">Srivijaya</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Kara-Khanid Khanate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> kingdom in Korea, and other countries that were also trade partners with <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999138_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999138-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall198523_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall198523-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESastri1984173.2C_316_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESastri1984173.2C_316-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen1996158_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen1996158-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrose2008258_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrose2008258-14">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_Song" title="History of Song">Chinese records</a> even mention <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">an embassy</a> from the ruler of "Fu lin" (拂菻, i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>), <a href="/wiki/Michael_VII_Doukas" title="Michael VII Doukas">Michael VII Doukas</a>, and its arrival in 1081.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-15">[14]</a></sup> However, China's closest neighbouring states had the greatest impact on its domestic and foreign policy. From its inception under Taizu, the Song dynasty alternated between warfare and diplomacy with the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitans</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao dynasty</a> in the northeast and with the <a href="/wiki/Tangut_people" title="Tangut people">Tanguts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a> in the northwest. The Song dynasty used military force in an attempt to quell the Liao dynasty and to recapture the <a href="/wiki/Sixteen_Prefectures" title="Sixteen Prefectures">Sixteen Prefectures</a>, a territory under Khitan control that was traditionally considered to be part of <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote199969_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote199969-16">[15]</a></sup> Song forces were repulsed by the Liao forces, who engaged in aggressive yearly campaigns into northern Song territory until 1005, when the signing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanyuan_Treaty" title="Shanyuan Treaty">Shanyuan Treaty</a> ended these northern border clashes. The Song were forced to provide tribute to the Khitans, although this did little damage to the Song economy since the Khitans were economically dependent upon importing massive amounts of goods from the Song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006154_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006154-17">[16]</a></sup> More significantly, the Song state recognized the Liao state as its diplomatic equal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote199970.E2.80.9371_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote199970.E2.80.9371-18">[17]</a></sup> The Song dynasty managed to win several military victories over the Tanguts in the early 11th century, culminating in a campaign led by the polymath scientist, general, and statesman <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031–1095).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin19958_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin19958-19">[18]</a></sup> However, this campaign was ultimately a failure due to a rival military officer of Shen disobeying direct orders, and the territory gained from the Western Xia was eventually lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin19959_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin19959-20">[19]</a></sup> There was also a significant war fought against the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BD_dynasty" title="Lý dynasty">Lý dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> from 1075 to 1077 over a border dispute and the Song's severing of commercial relations with the <a href="/wiki/%C4%90%E1%BA%A1i_Vi%E1%BB%87t" title="Đại Việt">Đại Việt</a> kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2008207_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2008207-21">[20]</a></sup> After Lý forces inflicted heavy damages in a raid on <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, the Song commander Guo Kui (1022–1088) penetrated as far as Thăng Long (modern <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2008208_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2008208-22">[21]</a></sup> Heavy losses on both sides prompted the Lý commander Thường Kiệt (1019–1105) to make peace overtures, allowing both sides to withdraw from the war effort; captured territories held by both Song and Lý were mutually exchanged in 1082, along with prisoners of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2008208.E2.80.93209_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2008208.E2.80.93209-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg"><img alt="An oval shaped pottery pillow with flat sides. It has an image of a white bird sitting on a branch." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1913" data-file-width="2551" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg/220px-Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg/330px-Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg/440px-Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oreiller_Guimet_291171.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cizhou_ware" title="Cizhou ware">Cizhou ware</a> pillow of Northern Song dynasty with incised decoration and iron-pigmented black slip with the image of a bird.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the 11th century, political rivalries divided members of the court due to the ministers' differing approaches, opinions, and policies regarding the handling of the Song's complex society and thriving economy. The idealist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_(China)" title="Chancellor (China)">Chancellor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fan_Zhongyan" title="Fan Zhongyan">Fan Zhongyan</a> (989–1052), was the first to receive a heated political backlash when he attempted to institute the <a href="/wiki/Qingli_Reforms" title="Qingli Reforms">Qingli Reforms</a>, which included measures such as improving the recruitment system of officials, increasing the salaries for minor officials, and establishing sponsorship programs to allow a wider range of people to be well educated and eligible for state service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006163_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006163-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Fan was forced to step down from his office, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Anshi" title="Wang Anshi">Wang Anshi</a> (1021–1086) became Chancellor of the imperial court. With the backing of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Shenzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Shenzong of Song">Emperor Shenzong</a> (1067–1085), Wang Anshi severely criticized the educational system and state bureaucracy. Seeking to resolve what he saw as state corruption and negligence, Wang implemented a series of reforms called the <a href="/wiki/New_Policies_(Song_dynasty)" title="New Policies (Song dynasty)">New Policies</a>. These involved <a href="/wiki/Land_value_tax" title="Land value tax">land value tax</a> reform, the establishment of several government <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolies</a>, the support of local <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militias</a>, and the creation of higher standards for the Imperial examination to make it more practical for men skilled in statecraft to pass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reforms created political factions in the court. Wang Anshi's "New Policies Group" (<i>Xin Fa</i>), also known as the "Reformers", were opposed by the ministers in the "Conservative" faction led by the historian and Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Sima_Guang" title="Sima Guang">Sima Guang</a> (1019–1086).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin19953.E2.80.934_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin19953.E2.80.934-26">[25]</a></sup> As one faction supplanted another in the majority position of the court ministers, it would demote rival officials and exile them to govern remote frontier regions of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164-25">[24]</a></sup> One of the prominent victims of the political rivalry, the famous poet and statesman <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a> (1037–1101), was jailed and eventually exiled for criticizing Wang's reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg"><img alt="A wooden carving of a sitting Buddhist figure in loose fitting, painted robes." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg/170px-Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg/255px-Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg/340px-Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liao_Dynasty_-_Guan_Yin_statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao</a> polychrome wood-carved statue of <a href="/wiki/Guanyin" title="Guanyin">Guanyin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, (907–1125)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the central Song court remained politically divided and focused upon its internal affairs, alarming new events to the north in the Liao state finally came to its attention. The <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a>, a subject tribe of the Liao, rebelled against them and formed their own state, the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty (1115–1234)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006165_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006165-27">[26]</a></sup> The Song official <a href="/wiki/Tong_Guan" title="Tong Guan">Tong Guan</a> (1054–1126) advised <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huizong_of_Song" title="Emperor Huizong of Song">Emperor Huizong</a> (1100–1125) to form an alliance with the Jurchens, and the joint military campaign under this <a href="/wiki/Alliance_Conducted_at_Sea" title="Alliance Conducted at Sea">Alliance Conducted at Sea</a> toppled and completely conquered the Liao dynasty by 1125.</p>
<p>However, the poor performance and military weakness of the Song army was observed by the Jurchens, who immediately broke the alliance, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Jin%E2%80%93Song_Wars" title="Jin–Song Wars">Jin–Song Wars</a> of 1125 and 1127. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingkang_Incident" title="Jingkang Incident">Jingkang Incident</a> during the latter invasion, the Jurchens captured not only the capital, but the retired emperor Huizong, his successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Qinzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Qinzong of Song">Emperor Qinzong</a>, and most of the Imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006165_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006165-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The remaining Song forces regrouped under the self-proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Song" title="Emperor Gaozong of Song">Emperor Gaozong of Song</a> (1127–1162) and withdrew south of the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> to establish a new capital at Lin'an (modern <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>). The Jurchen conquest of <a href="/wiki/North_China" title="North China">North China</a> and shift of capitals from Kaifeng to Lin'an was the dividing line between the Northern and Southern Song dynasties.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Song.2C_1127.E2.80.931279">Southern Song, 1127–1279</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Southern Song, 1127–1279">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg"><img alt="A map showing the territory of the Song dynasty after suffering losses to the Jin dynasty. The western and southern borders remain unchanged from the previous map, however the northernmost third of the Song's previous territory is now under control of the Jin. The Xia dynasty's territory generally remains unchanged. In the southwest, the Song dynasty is bordered by a territory about a sixth its size, Nanchao." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="825" data-file-width="840" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg/220px-China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg/330px-China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg/440px-China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:China_-_Southern_Song_Dynasty-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Southern Song in 1142</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although weakened and pushed south beyond the <a href="/wiki/Huai_River" title="Huai River">Huai River</a>, the Southern Song found new ways to bolster its strong economy and defend itself against the Jin dynasty. It had able military officers such as <a href="/wiki/Yue_Fei" title="Yue Fei">Yue Fei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_Shizhong" title="Han Shizhong">Han Shizhong</a>. The government sponsored massive <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harbor" title="Harbor">harbor</a> improvement projects, and the construction of <a href="/wiki/Beacon" title="Beacon">beacons</a> and seaport <a href="/wiki/Warehouse" title="Warehouse">warehouses</a> to support maritime trade abroad, including at the major international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaport" title="Seaport">seaports</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xiamen" title="Xiamen">Xiamen</a>, that were sustaining China's commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang200014_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang200014-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin19955_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin19955-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaludan1998136_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaludan1998136-30">[29]</a></sup> To protect and support the multitude of ships sailing for maritime interests into the waters of the <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sea" title="Yellow Sea">Yellow Sea</a> (to <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>), <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, it was necessary to establish an official standing <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen1996159.E2.80.93161_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen1996159.E2.80.93161-31">[30]</a></sup> The Song dynasty therefore established China's first permanent navy in 1132,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaludan1998136_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaludan1998136-30">[29]</a></sup> with a headquarters at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinghai" title="Dinghai">Dinghai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d476_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d476-32">[31]</a></sup> With a permanent navy, the Song were prepared to face the naval forces of the Jin on the Yangtze River in 1161, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tangdao" title="Battle of Tangdao">Battle of Tangdao</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Caishi" title="Battle of Caishi">Battle of Caishi</a>. During these battles the Song navy employed swift <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paddle_wheel_ship#East_Asia" title="Paddle wheel ship">paddle wheel driven</a> naval vessels armed with <a href="/wiki/Trebuchet" title="Trebuchet">trebuchet catapults</a> aboard the decks that launched gunpowder <a href="/wiki/Bomb" title="Bomb">bombs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d476_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d476-32">[31]</a></sup> Although the Jin forces commanded by <a href="/wiki/Wanyan_Liang" title="Wanyan Liang">Wanyan Liang (the Prince of Hailing)</a> boasted 70,000 men on 600 warships, and the Song forces only 3,000 men on 120 warships,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199443.E2.80.9347_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199443.E2.80.9347-33">[32]</a></sup> the Song dynasty forces were victorious in both battles due to the destructive power of the bombs and the rapid assaults by paddle wheel ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a134_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a134-34">[33]</a></sup> The strength of the navy was heavily emphasized after that. A century after the navy was founded it had grown in size to 52,000 fighting marines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d476_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d476-32">[31]</a></sup> The Song government confiscated portions of land owned by the landed gentry in order to raise revenue for these projects, an act which caused dissension and loss of loyalty amongst leading members of Song society but did not stop the Song's defensive preparations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006239_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006239-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmbreeGluck1997385_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmbreeGluck1997385-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200490.E2.80.9391_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200490.E2.80.9391-37">[36]</a></sup> Financial matters were made worse by the fact that many wealthy, land-owning families—some of which had officials working for the government—used their social connections with those in office in order to obtain tax-exempt status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198880_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198880-38">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg"><img alt="A wooden carving of a slightly overweight Buddha, sitting in a cross legged position. Clothing, including a shirt that covers the shoulders but leaves the chest exposed, and long, baggy pants, are carved into the statue." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg/170px-Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg/255px-Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg/340px-Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wood_Bodhisattva_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A seated wooden <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a> statue, <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a> (1115–1234)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the Song dynasty was able to hold back the Jin, a new foe came to power over the steppe, deserts, and plains north of the Jin dynasty. The <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> (r. 1206–1227), initially invaded the Jin dynasty in 1205 and 1209, engaging in large raids across its borders, and in 1211 an enormous Mongol army was assembled to invade the Jin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006235_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006235-39">[38]</a></sup> The Jin dynasty was forced to submit and pay tribute to the Mongols as <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassals</a>; when the Jin suddenly moved their capital city from <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> to Kaifeng, the Mongols saw this as a revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006236_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006236-40">[39]</a></sup> Under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei Khan</a> (r.1229–1241), both the Jin dynasty and Western Xia dynasty were conquered by Mongol forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006236_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006236-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a139_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a139-41">[40]</a></sup> The Mongols also invaded <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Korea" title="Mongol invasions of Korea">Korea</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Battle of Baghdad (1258)">Abbasid Caliphate</a> of the Middle East, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus" title="Mongol invasion of Rus">Kievan Rus'</a>. The Mongols were at one time allied with the Song, but this alliance was broken when the Song recaptured the former imperial capitals of Kaifeng, <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> at the collapse of the Jin dynasty. The Mongol leader <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a> led a campaign against the Song in 1259 but died on August 11 during the <a href="/wiki/Diaoyu_Fortress" title="Diaoyu Fortress">Battle of Diaoyu Fortress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006240_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006240-42">[41]</a></sup> Möngke's death and the ensuing succession crisis prompted <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu Khan</a> to pull the bulk of the Mongol forces out of the Middle East where they were poised to fight the <a href="/wiki/Bahri_dynasty" title="Bahri dynasty">Egyptian Mamluks</a> (who defeated the remaining Mongols at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Ain Jalut</a>). Although Hulagu was allied with <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, his forces were unable to help in the assault against the Song, due to Hulagu's war with the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198855.E2.80.9356_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198855.E2.80.9356-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kublai continued the assault against the Song, gaining a temporary foothold on the southern banks of the Yangtze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198849_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198849-44">[43]</a></sup> Kublai made preparations to take <a href="/wiki/Ezhou" title="Ezhou">Ezhou</a>, but a pending civil war with his brother <a href="/wiki/Ariq_B%C3%B6ke" title="Ariq Böke">Ariq Böke</a>—a rival claimant to the Mongol Khaganate—forced Kublai to move back north with the bulk of his forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198850.E2.80.9351_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198850.E2.80.9351-45">[44]</a></sup> In Kublai's absence, the Song forces were ordered by Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Jia_Sidao" title="Jia Sidao">Jia Sidao</a> to make an opportune assault, and succeeded in pushing the Mongol forces back to the northern banks of the Yangtze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198856_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198856-46">[45]</a></sup> There were minor border skirmishes until 1265, when Kublai won a significant battle in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198882_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198882-47">[46]</a></sup> From 1268 to 1273, Kublai blockaded the Yangtze River with his navy and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Xiangyang" title="Battle of Xiangyang">besieged Xiangyang</a>, the last obstacle in his way to invading the rich Yangtze River basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198882_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198882-47">[46]</a></sup> Kublai officially declared the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> in 1271. In 1275, a Song force of 130,000 troops under Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Jia_Sidao" title="Jia Sidao">Jia Sidao</a> was defeated by Kublai's newly appointed commander-in-chief, general <a href="/wiki/Bayan_of_the_Baarin" title="Bayan of the Baarin">Bayan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198888_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198888-48">[47]</a></sup> By 1276, most of the Song territory had been captured by Yuan forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a139_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a139-41">[40]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yamen" title="Battle of Yamen">Battle of Yamen</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a> in 1279, the Yuan army, led by the general <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Hongfan" title="Zhang Hongfan">Zhang Hongfan</a>, finally crushed the Song resistance. The last remaining ruler, the 8-year-old emperor <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Bing_of_Song" title="Emperor Bing of Song">Emperor Huaizong of Song</a>, committed suicide, along with Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Lu_Xiufu" title="Lu Xiufu">Lu Xiufu</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198894_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198894-49">[48]</a></sup> and 800 members of the royal clan. On Kublai's orders, carried out by his commander Bayan, the rest of the former imperial family of Song were unharmed; the deposed <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gong_of_Song" title="Emperor Gong of Song">Emperor Gong</a> was demoted, being given the title 'Duke of Ying', but was eventually exiled to <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> where he took up a monastic life. The former emperor would eventually be forced to commit suicide under the orders of Kublai's great-great grandson, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gegeen_Khan,_Emperor_Yingzong_of_Yuan" title="Gegeen Khan, Emperor Yingzong of Yuan">Gegeen Khan</a>, out of fear that Emperor Gong would stage a coup to restore his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198890_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198890-50">[49]</a></sup> Other members of the Song Imperial Family continued to live in the Yuan dynasty, including <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Mengfu" title="Zhao Mengfu">Zhao Mengfu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Yong" title="Zhao Yong">Zhao Yong</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Society of the Song dynasty">Society of the Song dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Culture of the Song dynasty">Culture of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_(I)_001.jpg"><img alt="A square painting of a branch with a cluster of white flowers at the end. The branch is superimposed over a red square with rounded edges." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1024" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28I%29_001.jpg/220px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28I%29_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28I%29_001.jpg/330px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28I%29_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28I%29_001.jpg/440px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28I%29_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_(I)_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The White Jasmine Branch</i>, early 12th-century painting; small paintings in the style of round-albums that captured realistic scenes of nature were widely popular in the Southern Song period.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Song dynasty<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> was an era of administrative sophistication and complex social organization. Some of the largest cities in the world were found in China during this period (Kaifeng and Hangzhou had populations of over a million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200689_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200689-53">[52]</a></sup> People enjoyed various social clubs and entertainment in the cities, and there were many schools and temples to provide the people with education and religious services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167-52">[51]</a></sup> The Song government supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_welfare" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a> programs including the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Retirement_home" title="Retirement home">retirement homes</a>, public <a href="/wiki/Clinic" title="Clinic">clinics</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauper" title="Pauper">paupers</a>' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graveyard" title="Graveyard">graveyards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167-52">[51]</a></sup> The Song dynasty supported a widespread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postal_administration" title="Postal administration">postal service</a> that was modeled on the earlier <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (202 BCE – CE 220) postal system to provide swift communication throughout the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d35_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d35-54">[53]</a></sup> The central government employed thousands of postal workers of various ranks to provide service for post offices and larger postal stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d36_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d36-55">[54]</a></sup> In rural areas, farming peasants either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landowner" title="Landowner">owned their own plots of land</a>, paid rents as <a href="/wiki/Tenant_farmer" title="Tenant farmer">tenant farmers</a>, or were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serf" title="Serf">serfs</a> on large estates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999155_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999155-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although women were on a lower social tier than men (according to <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian ethics</a>), they enjoyed many social and legal privileges and wielded considerable power at home and in their own small businesses. As Song society became more and more prosperous and parents on the bride's side of the family provided larger <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowries</a> for her marriage, women naturally gained many new legal rights in ownership of property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158-57">[56]</a></sup> Under certain circumstances, an unmarried daughter without brothers, or a surviving mother without sons, could inherit one-half of her father's share of undivided family property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> There were many notable and well-educated women, and it was a common practice for women to educate their sons during their earliest youth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200671_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200671-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin19951_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin19951-62">[61]</a></sup> The mother of the scientist, general, diplomat, and statesman Shen Kuo taught him essentials of military strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin19951_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin19951-62">[61]</a></sup> There were also exceptional women writers and poets, such as <a href="/wiki/Li_Qingzhao" title="Li Qingzhao">Li Qingzhao</a> (1084–1151), who became famous even in her lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg"><img alt="A landscape oriented parchment with twenty-one characters written vertically in seven columns." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="650" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg/220px-Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg/330px-Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg/440px-Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huang_Tingjian_Fu_Bo_Shen_Ci.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Calligraphy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Huang_Tingjian" title="Huang Tingjian">Huang Tingjian</a> (1045–1105), a renowned calligrapher and associate of <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China" title="Religion in China">Religion in China</a> during this period had a great effect on people's lives, beliefs, and daily activities, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a> on spirituality was popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006172_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006172-63">[62]</a></sup> The major deities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestral spirits</a>, and the many deities of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a> were worshipped with sacrificial offerings. Tansen Sen asserts that more <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">Buddhist monks</a> from <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> travelled to China during the Song than in the previous <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618–907).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200313_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200313-64">[63]</a></sup> With many ethnic foreigners travelling to China to conduct trade or live permanently, there came many foreign religions; religious minorities in China included <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Song_dynasty" title="Islam during the Song dynasty">Middle Eastern Muslims</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng_Jews" title="Kaifeng Jews">Kaifeng Jews</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Persian Manichaeans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet196282.E2.80.9383_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet196282.E2.80.9383-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d465_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d465-66">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The populace engaged in a vibrant social and domestic life, enjoying such public festivals as the <a href="/wiki/Lantern_Festival" title="Lantern Festival">Lantern Festival</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qingming_Festival" title="Qingming Festival">Qingming Festival</a>. There were entertainment quarters in the cities providing a constant array of amusements. There were puppeteers, acrobats, theatre actors, sword swallowers, snake charmers, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folklore" title="Chinese folklore">storytellers</a>, singers and musicians, prostitutes, and places to relax, including tea houses, restaurants, and organized banquets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-China-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962222.E2.80.93225_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962222.E2.80.93225-68">[67]</a></sup> People attended social clubs in large numbers; there were tea clubs, exotic food clubs, <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarian</a> and art collectors' clubs, horse-loving clubs, poetry clubs, and music clubs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006167-52">[51]</a></sup> Like regional cooking and cuisines in the Song, the era was known for its regional varieties of performing arts styles as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest199769.E2.80.9370_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest199769.E2.80.9370-69">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_opera" title="Chinese opera">Theatrical drama</a> was very popular amongst the elite and general populace, although <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a>—not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_Chinese" title="Vernacular Chinese">the vernacular language</a>—was spoken by actors on stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962223_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962223-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi1988162_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi1988162-71">[70]</a></sup> The four largest drama theatres in Kaifeng could hold audiences of several thousand each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest199776_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest199776-72">[71]</a></sup> There were also notable domestic pastimes, as people at home enjoyed activities such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_board_game" title="Go board game">go</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xiangqi" title="Xiangqi">xiangqi</a> board games.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_service_examinations_and_the_gentry">Civil service examinations and the gentry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Civil service examinations and the gentry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Society of the Song dynasty">Society of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_(III)_001.jpg"><img alt="A long, portrait oriented painting of a bare-chested, bearded man sitting on a mat under a tree, reading." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2070" data-file-width="1256" height="363" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_%28III%29_001.jpg/220px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_%28III%29_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_%28III%29_001.jpg/330px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_%28III%29_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_%28III%29_001.jpg/440px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_%28III%29_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_des_11._Jahrhunderts_(III)_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Scholar in a Meadow</i>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese painting</a> of the 11th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During this period greater emphasis was laid upon the <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a> system of recruiting officials; this was based upon degrees acquired through competitive <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">examinations</a>, in an effort to select the most capable individuals for governance. Selecting men for office through proven merit <a href="/wiki/Xiaolian" title="Xiaolian">was an ancient idea in China</a>. The civil service system became institutionalized on a small scale during the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> dynasties, but by the Song period it became virtually the only means for drafting officials into the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999145.E2.80.93146_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999145.E2.80.93146-73">[72]</a></sup> The advent of widespread <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> helped to widely circulate Confucian teachings and to educate more and more eligible candidates for the exams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147-74">[73]</a></sup> This can be seen in the number of exam takers for the low-level prefectural exams rising from 30,000 annual candidates in the early 11th century to 400,000 candidates by the late 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147-74">[73]</a></sup> The civil service and examination system allowed for greater <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_mobility" title="Social mobility">social mobility</a>, and equality in competition for those wishing to attain an official seat in government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162-75">[74]</a></sup> Using statistics gathered by the Song state, Edward A. Kracke, Sudō Yoshiyuki, and Ho Ping-ti supported the hypothesis that simply having a father, grandfather, or great-grandfather who had served as an official of state did not guarantee one would obtain the same level of authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982417.E2.80.93418_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982417.E2.80.93418-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHymes198635.E2.80.9336_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHymes198635.E2.80.9336-77">[76]</a></sup> Robert Hartwell and Robert P. Hymes criticized this model, stating that it places too much emphasis on the role of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> and considers only three paternal ascendants of exam candidates while ignoring the demographic reality of Song China, the significant proportion of males in each generation that had no surviving sons, and the role of the <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982417.E2.80.93418_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982417.E2.80.93418-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHymes198635.E2.80.9336_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHymes198635.E2.80.9336-77">[76]</a></sup> Many felt disenfranchised by what they saw as a bureaucratic system that favored the land-holding class able to afford the best education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162-75">[74]</a></sup> One of the greatest literary critics of this was the official and famous poet <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a>. Yet Su was a product of his times, as the identity, habits, and attitudes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrats" title="Scholar-bureaucrats">scholar-official</a> had become less <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocratic</a> and more <a href="/wiki/Bureaucrat" title="Bureaucrat">bureaucratic</a> with the transition of the periods from Tang to Song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159-78">[77]</a></sup> At the beginning of the dynasty, government posts were disproportionately held by two elite social groups: a founding elite who had ties with the founding emperor and a semi-hereditary professional elite who used long-held clan status, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_kinship" title="Chinese kinship">family connections</a>, and marriage alliances to secure appointments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982405.E2.80.93413_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982405.E2.80.93413-79">[78]</a></sup> By the late 11th century, the founding elite became obsolete, while political partisanship and factionalism at court undermined the marriage strategies of the professional elite, which dissolved as a distinguishable social group and was replaced by a multitude of gentry families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982416.E2.80.93420_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982416.E2.80.93420-80">[79]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg"><img alt="Five pieces of pale green pottery. From left to right, a cylindrical bowl with a large, flat rim, a vase with a long, thin stem and a short, almost spherical body, a tall, thin glass, a wider vase with no stem, and a small opening at the top, and a vase with a long, but wide stem and an almost spherical body." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1620" data-file-width="2838" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg/220px-Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg/330px-Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg/440px-Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Longquan_celadon" title="Longquan celadon">Longquan celadon</a> wares from <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, 13th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg"><img alt="An older woman sits in front of a machine consisting of a vertically aligned wheel composed of about two dozen flat wooden spokes, with a string for an outside rim. The wheel is held up by a simple wooden pole stand. Two other older women watch the first." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="864" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg/220px-Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg/330px-Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg/440px-Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wang_Juzheng%27s_Spinning_Wheel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Spinning Wheel</i>, a painting created by Northern Song artist Wang Juzheng, is one of the earliest representations of the invention</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to Song's enormous population growth and the body of its appointed scholar-officials being accepted in limited numbers (about 20,000 active officials during the Song period), the larger scholarly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gentry_(China)" title="Gentry (China)">gentry class</a> would now take over grassroots affairs on the vast local level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106-81">[80]</a></sup> Excluding the scholar-officials in office, this elite social class consisted of exam candidates, examination degree-holders not yet assigned to an official post, local tutors, and retired officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006101.E2.80.93106_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006101.E2.80.93106-82">[81]</a></sup> These learned men, degree-holders, and local elites supervised local affairs and sponsored necessary facilities of local communities; any local magistrate appointed to his office by the government relied upon the cooperation of the few or many local gentry in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106-81">[80]</a></sup> For example, the Song government—excluding the educational-reformist government under Emperor Huizong—spared little amount of state revenue to maintain <a href="/wiki/Zhou_(country_subdivision)" title="Zhou (country subdivision)">prefectural</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County_(People%27s_Republic_of_China)" title="County (People's Republic of China)">county</a> schools; instead, the bulk of the funds for schools was drawn from private financing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYuan1994196.E2.80.93199_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYuan1994196.E2.80.93199-83">[82]</a></sup> This limited role of government officials was a departure from the earlier Tang dynasty (618–907), when the government strictly regulated commercial markets and local affairs; now the government withdrew heavily from regulating commerce and relied upon a mass of local gentry to perform necessary duties in their communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The gentry distinguished themselves in society through their intellectual and antiquarian pursuits,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162.E2.80.93163_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006162.E2.80.93163-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999148_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999148-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006104_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006104-86">[85]</a></sup> while the homes of prominent landholders attracted a variety of <a href="/wiki/Courtier" title="Courtier">courtiers</a>, including artisans, artists, educational tutors, and entertainers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet196292.E2.80.9393_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet196292.E2.80.9393-87">[86]</a></sup> Despite the disdain for trade, commerce and the merchant class exhibited by the highly cultured and elite exam-drafted scholar-officials, <a href="/wiki/Commercialism" title="Commercialism">commercialism</a> played a prominent role in Song culture and society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-China-67">[66]</a></sup> A scholar-official would be frowned upon by his peers if he pursued means of profiteering outside of his official salary; however, this did not stop many scholar-officials from managing business relations through the use of intermediary agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet196260.E2.80.9361.2C_68.E2.80.9369_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet196260.E2.80.9361.2C_68.E2.80.9369-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law.2C_justice.2C_and_forensic_science">Law, justice, and forensic science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Law, justice, and forensic science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Society of the Song dynasty">Society of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_(II)_001.jpg"><img alt="A painting of a play being staged in a courtyard. In the centre, a man in loose black robes appears in mid-bow or mid-dance. To the left, two men dressed as guards are holding a third man, dressed in the same attire as the man in the centre. To the right, a heavy set man sits in a throne. Behind him stand three women in white face paint and a man dressed in the same attire as the man in the center." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1645" data-file-width="2024" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28II%29_001.jpg/220px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28II%29_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28II%29_001.jpg/330px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28II%29_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28II%29_001.jpg/440px-Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_%28II%29_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_des_12._Jahrhunderts_(II)_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Broken Balustrade</i>, early 12th-century painting</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Song <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judicial system</a> retained most of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_code" title="Legal code">legal code</a> of the earlier Tang dynasty, the basis of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_law" title="Traditional Chinese law">traditional Chinese law</a> up until the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006161_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006161-89">[88]</a></sup> Roving sheriffs maintained law and order in the municipal jurisdictions and occasionally ventured into the countryside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKnight1992155.E2.80.93157_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKnight1992155.E2.80.93157-90">[89]</a></sup> Official magistrates overseeing court cases were not only expected to be well-versed in written law but also to promote morality in society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006161_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006161-89">[88]</a></sup> Magistrates such as the famed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bao_Qingtian" title="Bao Qingtian">Bao Qingtian</a> (999–1062) embodied the upright, moral judge who upheld justice and never failed to live up to his principles. Song judges specified the guilty person or party in a criminal act and meted out punishments accordingly, often in the form of <a href="/wiki/Caning" title="Caning">caning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006161_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006161-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962107_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962107-91">[90]</a></sup> A guilty individual or parties brought to court for a criminal or civil offense were not viewed as wholly innocent until proven otherwise, while even accusers were viewed with a high level of suspicion by the judge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962107_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962107-91">[90]</a></sup> Due to costly court expenses and immediate jailing of those accused of criminal offences, people in the Song preferred to settle disputes and quarrels privately, without the court's interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962107_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962107-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shen Kuo's <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></i> argued against traditional Chinese beliefs in <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a> (such as his argument for two throat valves instead of three); this perhaps spurred the interest in the performance of post-mortem <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsies</a> in China during the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199530.E2.80.9331_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199530.E2.80.9331-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin1995pp._30.E2.80.9331.2C_footnote_27_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin1995pp._30.E2.80.9331.2C_footnote_27-93">[92]</a></sup> The physician and judge known as <a href="/wiki/Song_Ci" title="Song Ci">Song Ci</a> (1186–1249) wrote <a href="/wiki/Collected_Cases_of_Injustice_Rectified" title="Collected Cases of Injustice Rectified">a pioneering work</a> of <a href="/wiki/Forensic_science" title="Forensic science">forensic science</a> on the examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corpse" title="Corpse">corpses</a> in order to determine cause of death (strangulation, poisoning, drowning, blows, etc.) and to prove whether death resulted from murder, suicide, or accidental death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962170_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962170-94">[93]</a></sup> Song Ci stressed the importance of proper <a href="/wiki/Coroner" title="Coroner">coroner</a>'s conduct during autopsies and the accurate recording of the <a href="/wiki/Inquest" title="Inquest">inquest</a> of each autopsy by official clerks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESung198112.2C_72_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESung198112.2C_72-95">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_and_methods_of_warfare">Military and methods of warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Military and methods of warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Society of the Song dynasty">Society of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg"><img alt="A long, landscape oriented painting of eight figures. From left to right, the first, third, fifth, sixth, and eighth people are armed, with the first and last men most heavily armed, carrying both a bow and a sword, with the other three carrying either a bow or a sword but not both. Each of the men wears a thin, flat, black cap. The third and sixth figure are also a head shorter than the rest of the figures." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1174" data-file-width="4155" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg/300px-Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg/450px-Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg/600px-Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Four_Generals_of_Song.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Four Generals of Zhongxing" by Southern Song dynasty artist Liu Songnian (1174–1224); the renowned general <a href="/wiki/Yue_Fei" title="Yue Fei">Yue Fei</a> (1103–1142) is the second person from the left.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg/220px-Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg/330px-Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg/440px-Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dingzhou_Liaodi_Pagoda_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Liaodi_Pagoda" title="Liaodi Pagoda">Liaodi Pagoda</a>, the tallest pre-modern <a href="/wiki/Chinese_pagoda" title="Chinese pagoda">Chinese pagoda</a> built in 1055, was built as a <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_architecture" title="Buddhist architecture">Buddhist religious structure</a>, yet it served a military purpose as a <a href="/wiki/Watchtower" title="Watchtower">watchtower</a> for <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECai201181-82_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECai201181-82-96">[95]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Song military was chiefly organized to ensure that the army could not threaten Imperial control, often at the expense of effectiveness in war. Northern Song's Military Council operated under a Chancellor, who had no control over the imperial army. The imperial army was divided among three marshals, each independently responsible to the Emperor. Since the Emperor rarely led campaigns personally, Song forces lacked unity of command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBai2002239_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBai2002239-97">[96]</a></sup> The imperial court often believed that successful generals endangered royal authority, and relieved or even executed them (notably Li Gang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBai2002250_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBai2002250-98">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yue_Fei" title="Yue Fei">Yue Fei</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Han_Shizhong" title="Han Shizhong">Han Shizhong</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBai2002254.29._99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBai2002254.29.-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the scholar-officials viewed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_history_of_China_(pre-1911)" title="Military history of China (pre-1911)">military soldiers</a> as lower members in the hierarchic social order,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200225.E2.80.9326_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200225.E2.80.9326-100">[99]</a></sup> a person could gain status and prestige in society by becoming a high-ranking military officer with a record of victorious battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorge200543_101-0"><a
