href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorge200543-101">[100]</a></sup> At its height, the Song military had one million soldiers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164-25">[24]</a></sup> divided into <a href="/wiki/Platoon" title="Platoon">platoons</a> of 50 troops, companies made of two platoons, battalions composed of 500 soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorge200545_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorge200545-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130-103">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">Crossbowmen</a> were separated from the regular infantry and placed in their own units as they were prized combatants, providing effective missile fire against cavalry charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130-103">[102]</a></sup> The government was eager to sponsor new crossbow designs that could shoot at longer ranges, while crossbowmen were also valuable when employed as long-range <a href="/wiki/Sniper" title="Sniper">snipers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130.E2.80.93131_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130.E2.80.93131-104">[103]</a></sup> Song cavalry employed a slew of different weapons, including halberds, swords, bows, spears, and '<a href="/wiki/Fire_lance" title="Fire lance">fire lances</a>' that discharged a gunpowder blast of flame and <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(weaponry)" title="Fragmentation (weaponry)">shrapnel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeers2006131_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeers2006131-105">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Military strategy and military training were treated as science that could be studied and perfected; soldiers were tested in their skills of using weaponry and in their athletic ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeers2006129_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeers2006129-106">[105]</a></sup> The troops were trained to follow signal standards to advance at the waving of banners and to halt at the sound of bells and drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeers2006130-103">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Song navy was of great importance during the consolidation of the empire in the 10th century; during the war against the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Tang" title="Southern Tang">Southern Tang</a> state the Song navy employed tactics such as defending large floating <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">pontoon bridges</a> across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> in order to secure movements of troops and supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200287_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200287-107">[106]</a></sup> There were large naval ships in the Song that could carry 1,000 soldiers aboard their decks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286.E2.80.9387_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286.E2.80.9387-108">[107]</a></sup> while the swift-moving <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle-wheel craft</a> were viewed as essential fighting ships in any successful naval battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286.E2.80.9387_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286.E2.80.9387-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d422_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d422-109">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a battle on January 23, 971, massive arrow fire from Song dynasty crossbowmen decimated the <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephant</a> corps of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Han" title="Southern Han">Southern Han</a> army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer1957291_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer1957291-110">[109]</a></sup> This defeat not only marked the eventual submission of the Southern Han to the Song dynasty, but also the last instance where a war elephant corps was employed as a regular division within a Chinese army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer1957291_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer1957291-110">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was a total of 347 military treatises written during the Song period, as listed by the history text of the <i>Song Shi</i> (compiled in 1345).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e19_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e19-111">[110]</a></sup> However, only a handful of these military treatises have survived, which includes the <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing Zongyao</a></i> written in 1044. It was the first known book to have listed formulas for gunpowder;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e119_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e119-112">[111]</a></sup> it gave appropriate formulas for use in several different kinds of gunpowder bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e122.E2.80.93124_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e122.E2.80.93124-113">[112]</a></sup> It also provided detailed descriptions and illustrations of double-piston pump <a href="/wiki/Flamethrower" title="Flamethrower">flamethrowers</a>, as well as instructions for the maintenance and repair of the components and equipment used in the device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e82.E2.80.9384_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e82.E2.80.9384-114">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts.2C_literature.2C_and_philosophy">Arts, literature, and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Arts, literature, and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Culture of the Song dynasty">Culture of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Song_dynasty" title="Islam during the Song dynasty">Islam during the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg"><img alt="A wooden carving of a female human figure, sitting with one knee on the ground and one knee pointing up, with a hand resting on that knee. The carving includes loose fitting clothing, which is covered mainly in gold foil." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg/220px-Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg/330px-Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg/440px-Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wood_Bodhisattva.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A wooden <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a> statue from the Song dynasty (960–1279)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="441" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg/220px-Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg/330px-Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg/440px-Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fresco_of_the_Tomb_of_Chao_Ta-w%C3%AAng_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A fresco shows late Northern Sung dynasty women sitting at their dressing tables</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The visual arts during the Song dynasty were heightened by new developments such as advances in landscape and portrait painting. The gentry elite engaged in the arts as accepted pastimes of the cultured scholar-official, including <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">painting</a>, composing <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">poetry</a>, and writing <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">calligraphy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200681.E2.80.9383_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200681.E2.80.9383-115">[114]</a></sup> The poet and statesman Su Shi and his associate <a href="/wiki/Mi_Fu" title="Mi Fu">Mi Fu</a> (1051–1107) enjoyed antiquarian affairs, often borrowing or buying art pieces to study and copy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006163_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006163-24">[23]</a></sup> Poetry and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">literature</a> profited from the rising popularity and development of the <a href="/wiki/Ci_(poetry)" title="Ci (poetry)">ci poetry form</a>. Enormous encyclopedic volumes were compiled, such as works of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">historiography</a> and dozens of treatises on technical subjects. This included the <a href="/wiki/Universal_history" title="Universal history">universal history</a> text of the <i><a href="/wiki/Zizhi_Tongjian" title="Zizhi Tongjian">Zizhi Tongjian</a></i>, compiled into 1000 volumes of 9.4 million written <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a>. The genre of Chinese <a href="/wiki/Travel_literature" title="Travel literature">travel literature</a> also became popular with the writings of the geographer <a href="/wiki/Fan_Chengda" title="Fan Chengda">Fan Chengda</a> (1126–1193) and Su Shi, the latter of whom wrote the 'daytrip essay' known as <i><a href="/wiki/Su_Shi#Travel_record_literature" title="Su Shi">Record of Stone Bell Mountain</a></i> that used <a href="/wiki/Persuasive_writing" title="Persuasive writing">persuasive writing</a> to argue for a philosophical point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHargett198574.E2.80.9376_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHargett198574.E2.80.9376-116">[115]</a></sup> Although an early form of the local geographic <a href="/wiki/Gazetteer" title="Gazetteer">gazetteer</a> existed in China since the 1st century, the matured form known as "treatise on a place", or <i>fangzhi</i>, replaced the old "map guide", or <i>tujing</i>, during the Song dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBol200144_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBol200144-117">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The imperial courts of the emperor's palace were filled with his entourage of court painters, calligraphers, poets, and storytellers. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huizong_of_Song" title="Emperor Huizong of Song">Emperor Huizong</a> was a renowned artist as well as a patron of the arts. A prime example of a highly venerated court painter was <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zeduan" title="Zhang Zeduan">Zhang Zeduan</a> (1085–1145) who painted an enormous <a href="/wiki/Panoramic_painting" title="Panoramic painting">panoramic painting</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Along_the_River_During_the_Qingming_Festival" title="Along the River During the Qingming Festival">Along the River During the Qingming Festival</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Song" title="Emperor Gaozong of Song">Emperor Gaozong of Song</a> initiated a massive art project during his reign, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Eighteen_Songs_of_a_Nomad_Flute" title="Eighteen Songs of a Nomad Flute">Eighteen Songs of a Nomad Flute</a></i> from the life story of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cai_Wenji" title="Cai Wenji">Cai Wenji</a> (b. 177). This art project was a diplomatic gesture to the Jin dynasty while he negotiated for the release of his mother from Jurchen captivity in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999151_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999151-118">[117]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg"><img alt="A portrait of an older, balding man in a half pale green and half sky blue robe. He is sitting on an armchair holding a thin wooden stick, possibally a folded up fan." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1937" data-file-width="1576" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg/170px-Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg/255px-Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg/340px-Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinesischer_Maler_von_1238_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of the <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a> Buddhist monk <a href="/wiki/Wuzhun_Shifan" title="Wuzhun Shifan">Wuzhun Shifan</a>, painted in 1238.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Chinese Buddhism</a> had waned in influence but it retained its hold on the arts and on the charities of monasteries. Buddhism had a profound influence upon the budding movement of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Cheng_Yi_(philosopher)" title="Cheng Yi (philosopher)">Cheng Yi</a> (1033–1107) and <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> (1130–1200).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006168_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006168-119">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> Buddhism influenced Fan Zhongyan and Wang Anshi through its concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethical" title="Ethical">ethical</a> <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright195993_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright195993-120">[119]</a></sup> while Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> deeply affected the pre–Neo-Confucian doctrine of Cheng Yi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006168_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006168-119">[118]</a></sup> The philosophical work of Cheng Yi in turn influenced Zhu Xi. Although his writings were not accepted by his contemporary peers, Zhu's commentary and emphasis upon the Confucian classics of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Books" title="Four Books">Four Books</a> as an introductory corpus to Confucian learning formed the basis of the Neo-Confucian doctrine. By the year 1241, under the sponsorship of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Lizong_of_Song" title="Emperor Lizong of Song">Emperor Lizong</a>, Zhu Xi's Four Books and his commentary on them became standard requirements of study for students attempting to pass the civil service examinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006169_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006169-121">[120]</a></sup> The East Asian countries of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> also adopted Zhu Xi's teaching, known as the Shushigaku (朱子學, School of Zhu Xi) of Japan, and in Korea the Jujahak (주자학). Buddhism's continuing influence can be seen in painted artwork such as <a href="/wiki/Lin_Tinggui" title="Lin Tinggui">Lin Tinggui</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">Luohan</a> Laundering</i>. However, the ideology was highly criticized and even scorned by some. The statesman and historian <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xiu" title="Ouyang Xiu">Ouyang Xiu</a> (1007–1072) called the religion a "curse" that could only be remedied by uprooting it from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_China" title="Culture of China">Chinese culture</a> and replacing it with Confucian discourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright195988.E2.80.9389_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright195988.E2.80.9389-122">[121]</a></sup> A true revival of Buddhism in Chinese society would not occur until the Mongol rule of the Yuan dynasty, with Kublai Khan's sponsorship of <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drog%C3%B6n_Ch%C3%B6gyal_Phagpa" title="Drogön Chögyal Phagpa">Drogön Chögyal Phagpa</a> as the leading <a href="/wiki/Lama" title="Lama">lama</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> sect of <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a>, which had entered China in the Tang era, would also be revived in China under Mongol rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962215_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962215-123">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine_and_apparel">Cuisine and apparel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cuisine and apparel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Culture of the Song dynasty">Culture of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving,_Song_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="A red, square tray with cut off corners. Inlaid into the tray in gold foil is a design depicting two birds perched on a branch of a large, lowering plant." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="2060" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving%2C_Song_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving%2C_Song_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving%2C_Song_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving%2C_Song_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving%2C_Song_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving%2C_Song_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_lacquer_tray_with_gold_engraving,_Song_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A red <a href="/wiki/Lacquerware" title="Lacquerware">lacquerware</a> food tray with gold foil engraving designs of two long-tailed birds and a peony, dated 12th to early 13th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_law#China" title="Sumptuary law">Sumptuary laws</a> regulated the food that one consumed and the clothes that one wore according to status and social class. Clothing was made of hemp or cotton cloths, restricted to a colour standard of black and white. Trousers were the acceptable attire for peasants, soldiers, artisans, and merchants, although wealthy merchants might choose to wear more ornate clothing and male blouses that came down below the waist. Acceptable apparel for scholar-officials was rigidly defined by social ranking system. However, as time went on this rule of rank-graded apparel for officials was not as strictly enforced. Each official was able to display his awarded status by wearing different-coloured traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Chinese_clothing" title="Han Chinese clothing">silken robes</a> that hung to the ground around his feet, specific types of headgear, and even specific styles of girdles that displayed his graded-rank of officialdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962127.E2.80.9330_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962127.E2.80.9330-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Women wore long dresses, blouses that came down to the knee, skirts and jackets with long or short sleeves, while women from wealthy families could wear purple scarves around their shoulders. The main difference in women's apparel from that of men was that it was fastened on the left, not on the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962129_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962129-125">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main food staples in the diet of the lower classes remained rice, <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, and salted fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962134.E2.80.93137_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962134.E2.80.93137-126">[125]</a></sup> Song restaurant and tavern menus are recorded which list entrées for feasts, banquets, festivals, and carnivals. They reveal a diverse and lavish diet for those of the upper class. They could choose from a wide variety of meats and seafood, including shrimp, geese, duck, mussel, shellfish, <a href="/wiki/Fallow_deer" title="Fallow deer">fallow deer</a>, hare, partridge, pheasant, francolin, quail, fox, badger, clam, crab, and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962134.E2.80.93137_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962134.E2.80.93137-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198878_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198878-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest199773_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest199773-128">[127]</a></sup> Dairy products were rare in Chinese cuisine at this time. Beef was rarely consumed since the bull was a valuable draft animal, and dog meat was absent from the diet of the wealthy, although the poor could choose to eat dog meat if necessary (yet it was not part of their regular diet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962135.E2.80.93136_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962135.E2.80.93136-129">[128]</a></sup> People also consumed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Date_Palm" title="Date Palm">dates</a>, raisins, <a href="/wiki/Jujube" title="Jujube">jujubes</a>, pears, plums, apricots, pear juice, <a href="/wiki/Lychee" title="Lychee">lychee</a>-fruit juice, honey and ginger drinks, <a href="/wiki/Papaya" title="Papaya">pawpaw</a> juice, spices and seasonings of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_pepper" title="Sichuan pepper">Sichuan pepper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ginger" title="Ginger">ginger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetable_fats_and_oils" title="Vegetable fats and oils">oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sesame_oil" title="Sesame oil">sesame oil</a>, salt, and vinegar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198878_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198878-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest199786_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest199786-130">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy.2C_industry.2C_and_trade">Economy, industry, and trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Economy, industry, and trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Economy of the Song dynasty">Economy of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qingming_Festival_2.jpg"><img alt="A long, horizontally aligned section of painting that details several large trading barges docked along a river. The barges have curved bottomed hull and vertical wooden walls that are topped in a line of roofing tiles, but do not have actual roofs. Only one of the ships has a mast, although all of the ships do have large rudders." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="1596" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Qingming_Festival_2.jpg/300px-Qingming_Festival_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Qingming_Festival_2.jpg/450px-Qingming_Festival_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Qingming_Festival_2.jpg/600px-Qingming_Festival_2.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qingming_Festival_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">Chinese boats</a> from <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zeduan" title="Zhang Zeduan">Zhang Zeduan</a>'s (1085–1145) painting <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Along_the_River_During_Qingming_Festival" title="Along the River During Qingming Festival">Along the River During Qingming Festival</a></i>; Chinese ships of the Song period featured <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hulls</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bulkhead_(partition)" title="Bulkhead (partition)">watertight compartments</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Song dynasty had one of the most prosperous and advanced economies in the medieval world. Song Chinese invested their funds in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joint_stock_company" title="Joint stock company">joint stock companies</a> and in multiple sailing vessels at a time when monetary gain was assured from the vigorous overseas trade and domestic trade along the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">Grand Canal</a> and Yangtze River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006157_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006157-131">[130]</a></sup> Prominent merchant families and private businesses were allowed to occupy industries that were not already government-operated <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c23_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c23-132">[131]</a></sup> Both private and government-controlled industries met the needs of a growing Chinese population in the Song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006164-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c23_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c23-132">[131]</a></sup> Artisans and merchants formed <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> that the state had to deal with when assessing taxes, requisitioning goods, and setting standard worker's wages and prices on goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006157_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006157-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet196288.2C_94_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet196288.2C_94-133">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_industry" title="Iron industry">iron industry</a> was pursued by both private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneurs</a> who owned their own smelters as well as government-supervised smelting facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWagner2001178.E2.80.93179.2C_181.E2.80.93183_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner2001178.E2.80.93179.2C_181.E2.80.93183-134">[133]</a></sup> The Song economy was stable enough to produce over a hundred million kilograms (over two hundred million pounds) of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> product a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006158_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006158-135">[134]</a></sup> Large-scale <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in China would have continued if not for the 11th-century innovation of the use of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a> for smelting <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006158_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006158-135">[134]</a></sup> Much of this iron was reserved for military use in crafting weapons and armouring troops, but some was used to fashion the many iron products needed to fill the demands of the growing domestic market. The iron trade within China was advanced by the construction of new <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>, facilitating the flow of iron products from production centres to the large market in the capital city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmbreeGluck1997339_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmbreeGluck1997339-136">[135]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg"><img alt="Three pieces of white porcelain. From left to right, a vase with a grooved, fat body, a short, thin stem, and small opening with a large flat rim extending from it, a simple bowl with a thin brown trim around the outside rim, and a container in the shape of a granary, with several viable support posts, a tiled roof, and a staircase leading to a door, all built into the piece." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1093" data-file-width="1635" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg/220px-Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg/330px-Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg/440px-Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Song_Dynasty_Porcelain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Left item</i>: A Northern Song qingbai-ware vase with a transparent blue-toned <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">ceramic glaze</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Jingdezhen" title="Jingdezhen">Jingdezhen</a>, 11th century; <i>Center item</i>: A Northern or Southern Song qingbai-ware bowl with incised <a href="/wiki/Nelumbo_nucifera" title="Nelumbo nucifera">lotus</a> decorations, a metal rim, and a transparent blue-toned glaze, from Jingdezhen, 12th or 13th century; <i>Right item</i>: A Southern Song <a href="/wiki/Scale_model" title="Scale model">miniature model</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granary</a> with removable top lid and doorway, qingbai porcelain with transparent blue-toned glaze, Jingdezhen, 13th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The annual output of minted copper currency in 1085 reached roughly six billion coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156-5">[4]</a></sup> The most notable advancement in the Song economy was the establishment of the world's first government issued paper-printed money, known as <a href="/wiki/Jiaozi_(currency)" title="Jiaozi (currency)">Jiaozi</a> (see also <i><a href="/wiki/Huizi_(currency)" title="Huizi (currency)">Huizi</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156-5">[4]</a></sup> For the printing of <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">paper money</a>, the Song court established several government-run <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factories" title="Factories">factories</a> in the cities of <a href="/wiki/Huizhou" title="Huizhou">Huizhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Anqi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Anqi (page does not exist)">Anqi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e48_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e48-137">[136]</a></sup> The size of the workforce employed in paper money factories was large; it was recorded in 1175 that the factory at Hangzhou employed more than a thousand workers a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e48_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e48-137">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The economic power of Song China heavily influenced foreign economies abroad. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moroccan_people" title="Moroccan people">Moroccan</a> <a href="/wiki/Geographer" title="Geographer">geographer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Idrisi" title="Al-Idrisi">al-Idrisi</a> wrote in 1154 of the prowess of Chinese merchant ships in the Indian Ocean and of their annual voyages that brought iron, swords, silk, velvet, porcelain, and various textiles to places such as <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> in modern-day Iraq.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen1996159.E2.80.93161_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen1996159.E2.80.93161-31">[30]</a></sup> Foreigners, in turn, affected the Chinese economy. For example, many West Asian and Central Asian <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> went to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> to trade, becoming a preeminent force in the import and export industry, while some were even appointed as officers supervising economic affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d465_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d465-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Sea trade with the South-east Pacific, the Hindu world, the Islamic world, and East Africa brought merchants great fortune and spurred an enormous growth in the <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> industry of Song-era <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGolas1980_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGolas1980-139">[138]</a></sup> However, there was risk involved in such long overseas ventures. In order to reduce the risk of losing money on maritime trade missions abroad, wrote historians Ebrey, Walthall, and Palais:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>[Song era] investors usually divided their investment among many ships, and each ship had many investors behind it. One observer thought eagerness to invest in overseas trade was leading to an outflow of copper cash. He wrote, 'People along the coast are on intimate terms with the merchants who engage in overseas trade, either because they are fellow-countrymen or personal acquaintances....[They give the merchants] money to take with them on their ships for purchase and return conveyance of foreign goods. They invest from ten to a hundred strings of cash, and regularly make profits of several hundred percent'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology.2C_science.2C_and_engineering">Technology, science, and engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Technology, science, and engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Song dynasty">Science and technology of the Song dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Architecture of the Song dynasty">Architecture of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">List of Chinese inventions</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_discoveries" title="List of Chinese discoveries">List of Chinese discoveries</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gunpowder_warfare">Gunpowder warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gunpowder warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_gunpowder" title="History of gunpowder">History of gunpowder</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trebuchet1-intransit.jpg"><img alt="An ink on paper diagram of a trebuchet. A long arm with a spherical cap rests on top of a large square platform. The square platform is supported by four plain cut square beams, which connect to an open undercarriage. Rope hangs between the end of the pole that does not have the cap to the inside of the undercarriage, as far away from the start of the rope as possible. The assembly moves on four wheels attached to the sides of the undercarriage." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="308" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Trebuchet1-intransit.jpg/220px-Trebuchet1-intransit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/Trebuchet1-intransit.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trebuchet1-intransit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An illustration of a <a href="/wiki/Trebuchet" title="Trebuchet">trebuchet</a> catapult from the <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing Zongyao</a></i> manuscript of 1044. Trebuchets like this were used to launch the earliest type of explosive bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e117_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e117-140">[139]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Advancements in weapons technology enhanced by gunpowder, including the evolution of the early <a href="/wiki/Flamethrower" title="Flamethrower">flamethrower</a>, explosive <a href="/wiki/Grenade" title="Grenade">grenade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mine</a>, enabled the Song Chinese to ward off their militant enemies until the Song's ultimate collapse in the late 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e80_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e80-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e82_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e82-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e220.E2.80.93221_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e220.E2.80.93221-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e192_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e192-144">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossabi198879_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossabi198879-145">[144]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing Zongyao</a></i> manuscript of 1044 was the first book in history to provide formulas for gunpowder and their specified use in different types of bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e117_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e117-140">[139]</a></sup> While engaged in a war with the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, in 1259 the official Li Zengbo wrote in his <i>Kezhai Zagao, Xugaohou</i> that the city of <a href="/wiki/Qingzhou" title="Qingzhou">Qingzhou</a> was manufacturing one to two thousand strong iron-cased bomb shells a month, dispatching to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiangyang_District,_Xiangfan" title="Xiangyang District, Xiangfan">Xiangyang</a> and Yingzhou about ten to twenty thousand such bombs at a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e173.E2.80.93174_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e173.E2.80.93174-146">[145]</a></sup> In turn, the invading Mongols employed northern Chinese soldiers and used these same types of gunpowder weapons against the Song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e174.E2.80.93175_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e174.E2.80.93175-147">[146]</a></sup> By the 14th century the firearm and cannon could also be found in Europe, India, and the Islamic Middle East, during the early age of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gunpowder_warfare" title="Gunpowder warfare">gunpowder warfare</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Measuring_distance_and_mechanical_navigation">Measuring distance and mechanical navigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Measuring distance and mechanical navigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">As early as the Han dynasty</a>, when the state needed to effectively measure distances traveled throughout the empire, the Chinese relied on the mechanical <a href="/wiki/Odometer" title="Odometer">odometer</a> device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c283_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c283-148">[147]</a></sup> The Chinese odometer came in the form of a wheeled-carriage, its inner gears functioning off the rotated motion of the wheels, and specific units of distance—the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">li</a>—marked by the mechanical striking of a drum or bell for auditory alarm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c281.E2.80.93282_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c281.E2.80.93282-149">[148]</a></sup> The specifications for the 11th century odometer were written by Chief Chamberlain Lu Daolong, who is quoted extensively in the historical text of the <i>Song Shi</i> (compiled by 1345).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c283.E2.80.93284_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c283.E2.80.93284-150">[149]</a></sup> In the Song period, the odometer vehicle was also combined with another old complex mechanical device known as the <a href="/wiki/South-pointing_chariot" title="South-pointing chariot">south-pointing chariot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c291_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c291-151">[150]</a></sup> This device, originally crafted by <a href="/wiki/Ma_Jun" title="Ma Jun">Ma Jun</a> in the 3rd century, incorporated a <a href="/wiki/Differential_(mechanical_device)" title="Differential (mechanical device)">differential gear</a> that allowed a figure mounted on the vehicle to always point in the southern direction, no matter how the vehicle's wheels' turned about.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c287_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c287-152">[151]</a></sup> The device concept of the differential gear for this navigational vehicle is now found in modern <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a> in order to apply the equal amount of <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> to wheels rotating at different speeds.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymaths.2C_inventions.2C_and_astronomy">Polymaths, inventions, and astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Polymaths, inventions, and astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">List of Chinese inventions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG"><img alt="A diagram of the interior of a clock-tower. The clock mechanism has several large gears, however it is not apparent how they would receive stimulus to move." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1366" data-file-width="908" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG/170px-Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG/255px-Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG/340px-Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clock_Tower_from_Su_Song%27s_Book_desmear.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An interior diagram of the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_clock" title="Astronomical clock">astronomical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clocktower" title="Clocktower">clocktower</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng" title="Kaifeng">Kaifeng</a> featured in <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a>'s book, written by 1092 and published <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">in printed form</a> by the year 1094.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">Polymath</a> figures such as the statesmen <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a> (1020–1101) embodied advancements in all fields of study, including <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horology" title="Horology">horology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">pharmaceutical medicine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archeology" title="Archeology">archeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_criticism" title="Art criticism">art criticism</a>, and more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999148_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999148-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a136_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a136-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c446_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c446-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shen Kuo was the first to discern <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">magnetic declination</a> of <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">true north</a> while experimenting with a compass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMohn20031_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMohn20031-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmbreeGluck1997843_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmbreeGluck1997843-156">[155]</a></sup> Shen theorized that geographical <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climates gradually shifted</a> over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChanClanceyLoy200215_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChanClanceyLoy200215-157">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b614_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b614-158">[157]</a></sup> He created a theory of land formation involving concepts accepted in modern <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199523.E2.80.9324_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199523.E2.80.9324-159">[158]</a></sup> He performed optical experiments with <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a> just decades after <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> was the first to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c98_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c98-160">[159]</a></sup> He also improved the designs of astronomical instruments such as the widened astronomical <a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays#Astronomy" title="Dream Pool Essays">sighting tube</a>, which allowed Shen Kuo to fix the position of the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a> (which had shifted over centuries of time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199517_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199517-161">[160]</a></sup> Shen Kuo was also known for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> clockworks, as he invented a new overflow-tank <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">clepsydra</a> which had more efficient higher-order <a href="/wiki/Interpolation" title="Interpolation">interpolation</a> instead of linear interpolation in calibrating the measure of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199517_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199517-161">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Su Song was best known for his horology treatise written in 1092, which described and illustrated in great detail his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a>-powered, 12 metres (39 ft) tall <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_clock" title="Astronomical clock">astronomical</a> <a href="/wiki/Clock_tower" title="Clock tower">clock tower</a> built in Kaifeng. The clock tower featured large astronomical instruments of the <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celestial_globe" title="Celestial globe">celestial globe</a>, both driven by an early intermittently working <a href="/wiki/Escapement" title="Escapement">escapement</a> mechanism (similarly to the western <a href="/wiki/Verge_escapement" title="Verge escapement">verge escapement</a> of true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_clock" title="Mechanical clock">mechanical clocks</a> appeared in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> <a href="/wiki/Clock" title="Clock">clockworks</a> , derived from ancient clockworks of classical times).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c445_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c445-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c448_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c448-163">[162]</a></sup> Su's tower featured a rotating gear wheel with 133 clock jack <a href="/wiki/Mannequin" title="Mannequin">mannequins</a> who were timed to rotate past shuttered windows while <a href="/wiki/Striking_clock" title="Striking clock">ringing gongs and bells, banging drums</a>, and presenting announcement plaques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c165.2C_445_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c165.2C_445-164">[163]</a></sup> In his printed book, Su published a celestial atlas of five <a href="/wiki/Star_chart" title="Star chart">star charts</a>. These star charts feature a cylindrical projection similar to <a href="/wiki/Mercator_projection" title="Mercator projection">Mercator projection</a>, the latter being a cartographic innovation of <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a> in 1569.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d569_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d569-165">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b208_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b208-166">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_and_cartography">Mathematics and cartography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mathematics and cartography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mathematics" title="Chinese mathematics">Chinese mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_geography" title="Chinese geography">Chinese geography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png"><img alt="Inverted image of a stone rubbing, comprising a map of eastern China, complete with detailed rivers. The area of the map covered by land features a near perfect grid pattern, which because it does not overlap any text, is clearly the work of the original mapmaker." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="10117" data-file-width="9849" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png/220px-Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png/330px-Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png/440px-Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yuji_tu_-_enhanced_contrast.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i>Yu Ji Tu</i>, or "Map of the Tracks of Yu", carved into stone in 1137, located in the <a href="/wiki/Stele_Forest" title="Stele Forest">Stele Forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>. This 3 ft (0.91 m) squared map features a <a href="/wiki/Scale_(map)" title="Scale (map)">graduated scale</a> of 100 <a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">li</a> for each rectangular grid. China's coastline and river systems are clearly defined and precisely pinpointed on the map. <a href="/wiki/Yu_the_Great" title="Yu the Great">Yu</a> refers to the Chinese deity described in the <a href="/wiki/Yu_Gong" title="Yu Gong">geographical chapter</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Documents" title="Book of Documents">Book of Documents</a></i>, dated 5th–3rd centuries BCE.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were many notable improvements to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mathematics" title="Chinese mathematics">Chinese mathematics</a> during the Song era. Mathematician <a href="/wiki/Yang_Hui" title="Yang Hui">Yang Hui</a>'s 1261 book provided the earliest Chinese illustration of <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_triangle" title="Pascal's triangle">Pascal's triangle</a>, although it had earlier been described by Jia Xian in around 1100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b134.E2.80.93137_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b134.E2.80.93137-167">[166]</a></sup> Yang Hui also provided rules for constructing combinatorial arrangements in <a href="/wiki/Magic_square" title="Magic square">magic squares</a>, provided theoretical proof for <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>'s forty-third proposition about <a href="/wiki/Parallelogram" title="Parallelogram">parallelograms</a>, and was the first to use negative coefficients of 'x' in <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equation" title="Quadratic equation">quadratic equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b46.2C_59.E2.80.9360.2C_104_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b46.2C_59.E2.80.9360.2C_104-168">[167]</a></sup> Yang's contemporary <a href="/wiki/Qin_Jiushao" title="Qin Jiushao">Qin Jiushao</a> (c. 1202–1261) was the first to introduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/0_(number)" title="0 (number)">zero symbol</a> into Chinese mathematics;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b43_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b43-169">[168]</a></sup> before this blank spaces were used instead of zeroes in the system of <a href="/wiki/Counting_rods" title="Counting rods">counting rods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b62.E2.80.9363_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b62.E2.80.9363-170">[169]</a></sup> He is also known for working with the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_remainder_theorem" title="Chinese remainder theorem">Chinese remainder theorem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heron%27s_formula" title="Heron's formula">Heron's formula</a>, and astronomical data used in determining the <a href="/wiki/Winter_solstice" title="Winter solstice">winter solstice</a>. Qin's major work was the <i><a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Treatise_in_Nine_Sections" title="Mathematical Treatise in Nine Sections">Mathematical Treatise in Nine Sections</a></i> published in 1247.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">Geometry</a> was essential to <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_cartography" title="Chinese cartography">earliest extant Chinese maps</a> date to the 4th century BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu199390.E2.80.9393_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu199390.E2.80.9393-171">[170]</a></sup> yet it was not until the time of <a href="/wiki/Pei_Xiu" title="Pei Xiu">Pei Xiu</a> (224–271) that topographical <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a>, a formal <a href="/wiki/Grid_reference" title="Grid reference">rectangular grid</a> system, and use of a standard graduated scale of distances was applied to terrain maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu199396.E2.80.9397_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu199396.E2.80.9397-172">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b538.E2.80.93540_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b538.E2.80.93540-173">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions#R" title="List of Chinese inventions">Following a long tradition</a>, Shen Kuo created a <a href="/wiki/Raised-relief_map" title="Raised-relief map">raised-relief map</a>, while his other maps featured a uniform graduated scale of 1:900,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199522_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199522-174">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple1986179_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple1986179-175">[174]</a></sup> A 3 ft (0.91 m) squared map of 1137—carved into a stone block—followed a uniform grid scale of 100 li for each gridded square, and accurately mapped the outline of the coasts and river systems of China, extending all the way to India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986bpp._547.E2.80.93549.2C_Plate_LXXXI_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986bpp._547.E2.80.93549.2C_Plate_LXXXI-176">[175]</a></sup> Furthermore, the world's oldest known terrain map in printed form comes from the edited encyclopedia of Yang Jia in 1155, which displayed western China without the formal grid system that was characteristic of more professionally made Chinese maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986bp._549.2C_Plate_LXXXII_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986bp._549.2C_Plate_LXXXII-177">[176]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Gazetteer" title="Gazetteer">gazetteers</a> had existed since 52 CE during the Han dynasty and gazetteers accompanied by illustrative maps (Chinese: <i>tujing</i>) since the Sui dynasty, the illustrated gazetteer became much more common in the Song dynasty, when the foremost concern was for illustrative gazetteers to serve political, administrative, and military purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHargett1996406.2C_409.E2.80.93412_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHargett1996406.2C_409.E2.80.93412-178">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movable_type_printing">Movable type printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Movable type printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_printing_in_East_Asia" title="History of printing in East Asia">History of printing in East Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG"><img alt="A rectangular ink on paper diagram with several hundred dots, several of which are organized into constellations, such as a drawn bow (bottom center) and a tree (top left)." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="984" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/220px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/330px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/440px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of the <a href="/wiki/Star_chart" title="Star chart">star charts</a> from <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a>'s <i>Xin Yi Xiang Fa Yao</i> published in 1092, featuring cylindrical projection similar to <a href="/wiki/Mercator_projection" title="Mercator projection">Mercator projection</a> and the corrected position of the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a> thanks to Shen Kuo's astronomical observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d569_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d569-165">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b208_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b208-166">[165]</a></sup> Su Song's celestial atlas of five star maps is actually the oldest in <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printed</a> form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199532_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199532-179">[178]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The innovation of <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> printing was made by the artisan <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a> (990–1051), first described by the scientist and statesman Shen Kuo in his <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></i> of 1088.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e201.E2.80.93203_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e201.E2.80.93203-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199527_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199527-181">[180]</a></sup> The collection of Bi Sheng's original clay-fired <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">typeface</a> was passed on to one of Shen Kuo's nephews, and was carefully preserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199527_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199527-181">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c33_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c33-182">[181]</a></sup> Movable type enhanced the already widespread use of <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock methods of printing</a> thousands of documents and volumes of written literature, consumed eagerly by an increasingly literate public. The advancement of printing deeply affected education and the scholar-official class, since more books could be made faster while mass-produced, printed books were cheaper in comparison to laborious handwritten copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159-78">[77]</a></sup> The enhancement of widespread printing and <a href="/wiki/Print_culture" title="Print culture">print culture</a> in the Song period was thus a direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Social_mobility" title="Social mobility">social mobility</a> and expansion of the educated class of scholar elites, the latter which expanded dramatically in size from the 11th to 13th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159.E2.80.93160_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159.E2.80.93160-183">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>The movable type invented by Bi Sheng was ultimately trumped by the use of woodblock printing due to the limitations of the enormous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese character</a> writing system, yet movable type printing continued to be used and was improved in later periods. The <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> scholar-official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(official)" title="Wang Zhen (official)">Wang Zhen</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> 1290–1333) implemented a faster typesetting process, improved Bi's baked-clay movable type character set with a wooden one, and experimented with tin-metal movable type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e206.E2.80.93208.2C_217_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e206.E2.80.93208.2C_217-184">[183]</a></sup> The wealthy printing patron <a href="/wiki/Hua_Sui" title="Hua Sui">Hua Sui</a> (1439–1513) of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> established China's first metal movable type (using bronze) in 1490.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e212.E2.80.93213_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e212.E2.80.93213-185">[184]</a></sup> In 1638 the <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> <a href="/wiki/Gazette" title="Gazette">Gazette</a> switched their printing process from woodblock to movable type printing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998xxi_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998xxi-186">[185]</a></sup> Yet it was during the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> that massive printing projects began to employ movable type printing. This includes the printing of sixty-six copies of a 5,020 volume long encyclopedia in 1725, the <i><a href="/wiki/Gujin_Tushu_Jicheng" title="Gujin Tushu Jicheng">Gujin Tushu Jicheng</a></i> (<i>Complete Collection of Illustrations and Writings from the Earliest to Current Times</i>), which necessitated the crafting of 250,000 movable type characters cast in bronze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e215.E2.80.93216_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e215.E2.80.93216-187">[186]</a></sup> By the 19th century the European style <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> replaced the old Chinese methods of movable type, while traditional woodblock printing in modern East Asia is used sparsely and for aesthetic reasons.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydraulic_and_nautical_engineering">Hydraulic and nautical engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hydraulic and nautical engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Song dynasty">Science and technology of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<p>The most important nautical innovation of the Song period seems to have been the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetic</a> mariner's <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>, which permitted accurate <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a> on the open sea regardless of the weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESivin199522_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESivin199522-174">[173]</a></sup> The magnetized compass needle – known in Chinese as the "south-pointing needle" – was first described by <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> in his 1088 <i>Dream Pool Essays</i> and first mentioned in active use by sailors in <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Yu_(author)" title="Zhu Yu (author)">Zhu Yu</a>'s 1119 <i>Pingzhou Table Talks</i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canal_lock.svg"><img alt="A diagram of the pound lock system, from a bird's eye perspective and from a side perspective. The bird's eye view illustrates that water enters the enclosed area through two culverts on either side of the upper lock gate. The side view diagram illustrates how the elevation is higher before reaching the top gate than it is afterwards." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="900" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Canal_lock.svg/200px-Canal_lock.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Canal_lock.svg/300px-Canal_lock.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Canal_lock.svg/400px-Canal_lock.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canal_lock.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plan and side view of a canal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound_lock" title="Pound lock">pound lock</a>, a concept pioneered in 984 by the Assistant Commissioner of Transport for <a href="/wiki/Huainan" title="Huainan">Huainan</a>, the engineer Qiao Weiyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d350_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d350-188">[187]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were other considerable advancements in <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_engineering" title="Hydraulic engineering">hydraulic engineering</a> and nautical technology during the Song dynasty. The 10th-century invention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound_lock" title="Pound lock">pound lock</a> for canal systems allowed different water levels to be raised and lowered for separated segments of a canal, which significantly aided the safety of canal traffic and allowed for larger barges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d350.E2.80.93351_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d350.E2.80.93351-189">[188]</a></sup> There was the Song-era innovation of <a href="/wiki/Bulkhead_(partition)" title="Bulkhead (partition)">watertight bulkhead compartments</a> that allowed damage to <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hulls</a> without sinking the ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d463_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d463-190">[189]</a></sup> If ships were damaged, the Chinese of the 11th century employed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock" title="Drydock">drydocks</a> to repair them while suspended out of the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d660_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d660-191">[190]</a></sup> The Song used crossbeams to brace the ribs of ships in order to strengthen them in a skeletal-like structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286-192">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">Stern</a>-mounted <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudders</a> had been mounted on Chinese ships since the 1st century, as evidenced with a preserved Han tomb model of a ship. In the Song period, the Chinese devised a way to mechanically raise and lower rudders in order for ships to travel in a wider range of water depths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286-192">[191]</a></sup> The Song arranged the protruding teeth of anchors in a circular pattern instead of in one direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286-192">[191]</a></sup> David Graff and Robin Higham state that this arrangement "[made] them more reliable" for anchoring ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286_192-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraffHigham200286-192">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_engineering_and_architecture">Structural engineering and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Structural engineering and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Architecture of the Song dynasty">Architecture of the Song dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg"><img alt="A wide, octagonal pagoda. It has four tall, functional floors made of brick, and an additional five, short, purely decorative floors made of wood. Each floor is separated by an eave, and the top five floor's eaves look as if they were simply stacked right on top of one another." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg/170px-Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg/255px-Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg/340px-Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhengding_Lingxiao_Pagoda_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 42-metre (138 ft) tall, brick and wood <a href="/wiki/Lingxiao_Pagoda" title="Lingxiao Pagoda">Lingxiao Pagoda</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengding" title="Zhengding">Zhengding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>, built in 1045.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Architecture during the Song period reached new heights of sophistication. Authors such as <a href="/wiki/Yu_Hao" title="Yu Hao">Yu Hao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> wrote books outlining the field of architectural layouts, craftsmanship, and <a href="/wiki/Structural_engineering" title="Structural engineering">structural engineering</a> in the 10th and 11th centuries, respectively. Shen Kuo preserved the written dialogues of Yu Hao when describing technical issues such as slanting <a href="/wiki/Strut" title="Strut">struts</a> built into pagoda towers for diagonal wind bracing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d141_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d141-193">[192]</a></sup> Shen Kuo also preserved Yu's specified dimensions and units of measurement for various building types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d82.E2.80.9384_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d82.E2.80.9384-194">[193]</a></sup> The architect <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Song_dynasty#Treatise_of_Li_Jie" title="Architecture of the Song dynasty">Li Jie</a> (1065–1110), who published the <i><a href="/wiki/Yingzao_Fashi" title="Yingzao Fashi">Yingzao Fashi</a></i> ('Treatise on Architectural Methods') in 1103, greatly expanded upon the works of Yu Hao and compiled the standard building codes used by the central government agencies and by craftsmen throughout the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19984.E2.80.936_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19984.E2.80.936-195">[194]</a></sup> He addressed the standard methods of construction, design, and applications of moats and fortifications, stonework, greater woodwork, lesser woodwork, wood-carving, turning and drilling, sawing, bamboo work, tiling, wall building, painting and decoration, brickwork, glazed tile making, and provided proportions for <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> formulas in <a href="/wiki/Masonry" title="Masonry">masonry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d85_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d85-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19985_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19985-197">[196]</a></sup> In his book, Li provided detailed and vivid illustrations of architectural components and cross-sections of buildings. These illustrations displayed various applications of <a href="/wiki/Corbel" title="Corbel">corbel</a> brackets, <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> arms, <a href="/wiki/Mortise_and_tenon" title="Mortise and tenon">mortise and tenon</a> work of tie beams and cross beams, and diagrams showing the various building types of halls in graded sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d96.E2.80.93100.2C_108.E2.80.93109_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d96.E2.80.93100.2C_108.E2.80.93109-198">[197]</a></sup> He also outlined the standard units of measurement and standard dimensional measurements of all building components described and illustrated in his book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19981.E2.80.936_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19981.E2.80.936-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg"><img alt="A painting of people boating in a lake.
