There is a small island in the centre of the lake, connected to the mainland by an arched bridge. The entire lake is surrounded by a low wall." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="591" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg/220px-Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg/330px-Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg/440px-Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Song-Imperial-Garden1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Games in the Jinming Pool</i>, silk painting by <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zeduan" title="Zhang Zeduan">Zhang Zeduan</a>, depiction of <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng" title="Kaifeng">Kaifeng</a>, Northern Song era.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grandiose building projects were supported by the government, including the erection of towering Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Chinese_pagoda" title="Chinese pagoda">Chinese pagodas</a> and the construction of enormous bridges (wood or stone, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trestle" title="Trestle">trestle</a> or segmental <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arch bridge</a>). Many of the pagoda towers built during the Song period were erected at heights that exceeded ten stories. Some of the most famous are the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Pagoda" title="Iron Pagoda">Iron Pagoda</a> built in 1049 during the Northern Song and the <a href="/wiki/Liuhe_Pagoda" title="Liuhe Pagoda">Liuhe Pagoda</a> built in 1165 during the Southern Song, although <a href="/wiki/Category:Chinese_pagodas" title="Category:Chinese pagodas">there were many others</a>. The tallest is the <a href="/wiki/Liaodi_Pagoda" title="Liaodi Pagoda">Liaodi Pagoda</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a> built in 1055, towering 84 m (276 ft) in total height. Some of the bridges reached lengths of 1,220 m (4,000 ft), with many being wide enough to allow two lanes of cart traffic simultaneously over a waterway or ravine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d151.E2.80.93153_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d151.E2.80.93153-200">[199]</a></sup> The government also oversaw construction of their own administrative offices, palace apartments, city fortifications, ancestral temples, and Buddhist temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d84_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d84-201">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>The professions of the architect, craftsman, carpenter, and structural engineer were not seen as professionally equal to that of a Confucian scholar-official. Architectural knowledge had been passed down orally for thousands of years in China, in many cases from a father craftsman to his son. Structural engineering and architecture schools were known to have existed during the Song period; one prestigious engineering school was headed by the renowned bridge-builder <a href="/wiki/Cai_Xiang" title="Cai Xiang">Cai Xiang</a> (1012–1067) in medieval <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d153_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d153-202">[201]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG"><img alt="A diagram showing multiple elaborately carved triangular brackets attached to each of the vertical support beams inside of a building." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1087" data-file-width="837" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG/220px-Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG/330px-Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG/440px-Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bracket_(architecture)" title="Bracket (architecture)">Bracket</a> arm clusters containing <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilevers</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Song_dynasty#Literature" title="Architecture of the Song dynasty">Li Jie's</a> building manual <i><a href="/wiki/Yingzao_Fashi" title="Yingzao Fashi">Yingzao Fashi</a></i>, printed in 1103.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Besides existing buildings and technical literature of building manuals, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Song_dynasty#Architecture_in_Song_artwork" title="Architecture of the Song dynasty">Song dynasty artwork</a> portraying <a href="/wiki/Cityscape" title="Cityscape">cityscapes</a> and other buildings aid modern-day scholars in their attempts to reconstruct and realize the nuances of Song architecture. Song dynasty artists such as <a href="/wiki/Li_Cheng_(painter)" title="Li Cheng (painter)">Li Cheng</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fan_Kuan" title="Fan Kuan">Fan Kuan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guo_Xi" title="Guo Xi">Guo Xi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zeduan" title="Zhang Zeduan">Zhang Zeduan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huizong_of_Song" title="Emperor Huizong of Song">Emperor Huizong of Song</a>, and Ma Lin painted close-up depictions of buildings as well as large expanses of cityscapes featuring <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arched bridges</a>, halls and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_pavilion" title="Chinese pavilion">pavilions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagoda towers</a>, and distinct <a href="/wiki/Chinese_city_wall" title="Chinese city wall">Chinese city walls</a>. The scientist and statesman Shen Kuo was known for his <a href="/wiki/Art_criticism" title="Art criticism">criticism of artwork</a> relating to architecture, saying that it was more important for an artist to capture a holistic view of a landscape than it was to focus on the angles and corners of buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d115_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d115-203">[202]</a></sup> For example, Shen criticized the work of the painter Li Cheng for failing to observe the principle of "seeing the small from the viewpoint of the large" in portraying buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d115_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d115-203">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were also pyramidal tomb structures in the Song era, such as the Song imperial tombs located in Gongxian, <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinhardt1993375_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinhardt1993375-204">[203]</a></sup> About 100 km (62 mi) from Gongxian is another Song dynasty tomb at Baisha, which features "elaborate facsimiles in brick of Chinese timber frame construction, from door lintels to pillars and pedestals to bracket sets, that adorn interior walls."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinhardt1993375_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinhardt1993375-204">[203]</a></sup> The two large chambers of the Baisha tomb also feature conical-shaped roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinhardt1993376_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinhardt1993376-205">[204]</a></sup> Flanking the avenues leading to these tombs <a href="/wiki/File:Songling2.jpg" title="File:Songling2.jpg">are lines of Song dynasty stone statues</a> of officials, tomb guardians, animals, and mythological creatures.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">Archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Song_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Chinese_archaeology" title="History of Chinese archaeology">History of Chinese archaeology</a></div>
<p>In addition to the Song gentry's antiquarian pursuits of art collecting, scholar-officials during the Song became highly interested in retrieving ancient relics from <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> sites, in order to revive the use of ancient vessels in ceremonies of state ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227-206">[205]</a></sup> Scholar-officials of the Song period claimed to have discovered ancient bronze vessels that were created as far back as the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> (1600–1046 BCE), which bore the <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">written characters of the Shang era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200633_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200633-207">[206]</a></sup> Some attempted to recreate these bronze vessels by using imagination alone, not by observing tangible evidence of relics; this practice was criticized by Shen Kuo in his work of 1088.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227-206">[205]</a></sup> Yet Shen Kuo had much more to criticize than this practice alone. Shen objected to the idea of his peers that ancient relics were products created by famous "sages" in lore or the <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">ancient aristocratic class</a>; Shen rightfully attributed the discovered handicrafts and vessels from ancient times as the work of artisans and commoners from previous eras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227_206-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227-206">[205]</a></sup> He also disapproved of his peers' pursuit of archaeology simply to enhance state ritual, since Shen not only took an <a href="/wiki/Interdisciplinarity" title="Interdisciplinarity">interdisciplinary</a> approach with the study of archaeology, but he also emphasized the study of functionality and investigating what was the ancient relics' original processes of manufacture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227_206-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227-206">[205]</a></sup> Shen used ancient texts and existing models of <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary spheres</a> to create one based on ancient standards; Shen described ancient weaponry such as the use of a <a href="/wiki/Sight_(device)" title="Sight (device)">scaled sighting device</a> on crossbows; while experimenting with <a href="/wiki/Bar_(music)" title="Bar (music)">ancient musical measures</a>, Shen suggested hanging <a href="/wiki/Bianzhong" title="Bianzhong">an ancient bell</a> by using a hollow handle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227_206-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraserHaber1986227-206">[205]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liu_Ding.jpg"><img alt="A heavily tarnished bronze bowl adorned with several carvings of squares that curl in on themselves at the bottom. It has three stubby, unadorned legs and two small, square handles coming off from the top rim." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1901" data-file-width="1881" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Liu_Ding.jpg/220px-Liu_Ding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Liu_Ding.jpg/330px-Liu_Ding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Liu_Ding.jpg/440px-Liu_Ding.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liu_Ding.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scholars of the Song claim to have collected ancient relics dating back as far as the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, such as this <a href="/wiki/Ding_(vessel)" title="Ding (vessel)">bronze <i>ding</i> vessel</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the gentry's overriding interest in archaeology simply for reviving ancient state rituals, some of Shen's peers took a similar approach to the study of archaeology. His contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xiu" title="Ouyang Xiu">Ouyang Xiu</a> (1007–1072) compiled an analytical catalogue of ancient rubbings on stone and bronze which pioneered ideas in early <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a> and archaeology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999148_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999148-85">[84]</a></sup> During the 11th century, Song scholars discovered the ancient shrine of Wu Liang (78–151 CE), a scholar of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (202 BCE – 220 CE); they produced rubbings of the carvings and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief">bas-reliefs</a> decorating the walls of his tomb so that they could be analyzed elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000142_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000142-208">[207]</a></sup> On the unreliability of historical works written after the fact, scholar-official <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Mingcheng" title="Zhao Mingcheng">Zhao Mingcheng</a> (1081–1129) stated "...the inscriptions on stone and bronze are made at the time the events took place and can be trusted without reservation, and thus discrepancies may be discovered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolph1963170_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolph1963170-209">[208]</a></sup> Historian R.C. Rudolph states that Zhao's emphasis on consulting contemporary sources for accurate dating is parallel with the concern of the German historian <a href="/wiki/Leopold_von_Ranke" title="Leopold von Ranke">Leopold von Ranke</a> (1795–1886),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolph1963170_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolph1963170-209">[208]</a></sup> and was in fact emphasized by many Song scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolph1963172_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolph1963172-210">[209]</a></sup> The Song scholar Hong Mai (1123–1202) heavily criticized what he called the court's "ridiculous" archaeological catalogue <i>Bogutu</i> compiled during the Huizong reign periods of Zheng He and Xuan He (1111–1125).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolph1963170.E2.80.93171_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolph1963170.E2.80.93171-211">[210]</a></sup> Hong Mai obtained old vessels from the Han dynasty and compared them with the descriptions offered in the catalogue, which he found so inaccurate he stated he had to "hold my sides with laughter."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolph1963171_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolph1963171-212">[211]</a></sup> Hong Mai pointed out that the erroneous material was the fault of Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Cai_Jing" title="Cai Jing">Cai Jing</a> (1047–1126), who prohibited scholars from reading and consulting the written histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolph1963171_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolph1963171-212">[211]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
